</td></tr><tr style="display:none;"><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></td></tr><tr style="padding: 0 0.6em 0.2em 0.6em;line-height: 1.2em;"><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Discharge</th><td class="infobox-data"> </td></tr><tr style="padding: 0 0.6em 0.2em 0.6em;line-height: 1.2em;"><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><span style="font-weight:normal"> • location</span></th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Cairo" title="Cairo">Cairo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a></td></tr><tr style="padding: 0 0.6em 0.2em 0.6em;line-height: 1.2em;"><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><span style="font-weight:normal"> • average</span></th><td class="infobox-data">2,000 m<sup>3</sup>/s (71,000 cu ft/s)</td></tr><tr style="padding: 0 0.6em 0.2em 0.6em;line-height: 1.2em;"><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><span style="font-weight:normal"> • minimum</span></th><td class="infobox-data">500 m<sup>3</sup>/s (18,000 cu ft/s)</td></tr><tr style="padding: 0 0.6em 0.2em 0.6em;line-height: 1.2em;"><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><span style="font-weight:normal"> • maximum</span></th><td class="infobox-data">7,000 m<sup>3</sup>/s (250,000 cu ft/s)</td></tr><tr style="display:none"><td colspan="2">
</td></tr><tr style="display:none;"><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></td></tr><tr style="padding: 0 0.6em 0.2em 0.6em;line-height: 1.2em;"><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Discharge</th><td class="infobox-data"> </td></tr><tr style="padding: 0 0.6em 0.2em 0.6em;line-height: 1.2em;"><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><span style="font-weight:normal"> • location</span></th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Nile_Delta" title="Nile Delta">Nile Delta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a></td></tr><tr style="padding: 0 0.6em 0.2em 0.6em;line-height: 1.2em;"><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><span style="font-weight:normal"> • average</span></th><td class="infobox-data">1,584 m<sup>3</sup>/s (55,900 cu ft/s)</td></tr><tr style="display:none"><td colspan="2">
</td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-header" colspan="2" style="background-color: #CEDEFF;">Basin features</th></tr><tr style="padding: 0 0.6em 0.2em 0.6em;line-height: 1.2em;"><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Tributaries</th><td class="infobox-data"> </td></tr><tr style="padding: 0 0.6em 0.2em 0.6em;line-height: 1.2em;"><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><span style="font-weight:normal"> • left</span></th><td class="infobox-data"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_Nile_River" title="White Nile River">White Nile</a></td></tr><tr style="padding: 0 0.6em 0.2em 0.6em;line-height: 1.2em;"><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><span style="font-weight:normal"> • right</span></th><td class="infobox-data"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blue_Nile_River" title="Blue Nile River">Blue Nile</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atbara_River" title="Atbara River">Atbara</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>Nile</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[b]</a></sup> is a major north-flowing <a href="/wiki/River" title="River">river</a> in northeastern <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>. It flows into the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>. The Nile is the longest river in Africa and has historically been considered the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_rivers_by_length" title="List of rivers by length">longest river in the world</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica-nile_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-nile-5">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-liu-etal-river-length_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-liu-etal-river-length-6">[4]</a></sup> though this has been contested by research suggesting that the <a href="/wiki/Amazon_River" title="Amazon River">Amazon River</a> is slightly longer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AmazonRiver_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-AmazonRiver-7">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica-amazon-length_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-amazon-length-8">[6]</a></sup> Of the world's major rivers, the Nile is one of the smallest, as measured by annual flow in cubic metres of water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Said_3_x_3_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Said_3_x_3-9">[7]</a></sup> About 6,650 km (4,130 mi)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-length_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-length-1">[a]</a></sup> long, its <a href="/wiki/Drainage_basin" title="Drainage basin">drainage basin</a> covers eleven countries: the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">Democratic Republic of the Congo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Burundi" title="Burundi">Burundi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rwanda" title="Rwanda">Rwanda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eritrea" title="Eritrea">Eritrea</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Sudan" title="South Sudan">South Sudan</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Republic_of_the_Sudan" title="Republic of the Sudan">Republic of the Sudan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[9]</a></sup> In particular, the Nile is the primary water source of Egypt, Sudan and South Sudan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hdl.handle_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-hdl.handle-12">[10]</a></sup> Additionally, the Nile is an important economic river, supporting agriculture and fishing.
</p><p>The Nile has two major <a href="/wiki/Tributary" title="Tributary">tributaries</a> – the <a href="/wiki/White_Nile" title="White Nile">White Nile</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Blue_Nile" title="Blue Nile">Blue Nile</a>. The White Nile is traditionally considered to be the <a href="/wiki/River_source" title="River source">headwaters</a> <a href="/wiki/Stream" title="Stream">stream</a>. However, the Blue Nile is the source of most of the water of the Nile downstream, containing 80% of the water and <a href="/wiki/Silt" title="Silt">silt</a>. The White Nile is longer and rises in the <a href="/wiki/African_Great_Lakes" title="African Great Lakes">Great Lakes</a> region. It begins at <a href="/wiki/Lake_Victoria" title="Lake Victoria">Lake Victoria</a> and flows through Uganda and South Sudan. The Blue Nile begins at <a href="/wiki/Lake_Tana" title="Lake Tana">Lake Tana</a> in Ethiopia<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[11]</a></sup> and flows into Sudan from the southeast. The two <a href="/wiki/Confluence" title="Confluence">rivers meet</a> at the Sudanese capital of <a href="/wiki/Khartoum" title="Khartoum">Khartoum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>The northern section of the river flows north almost entirely through the <a href="/wiki/Nubian_Desert" title="Nubian Desert">Nubian Desert</a> to <a href="/wiki/Cairo" title="Cairo">Cairo</a> and its <a href="/wiki/Nile_Delta" title="Nile Delta">large delta</a>, and the river flows into the Mediterranean Sea at <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a>. <a href="/wiki/History_of_Egypt" title="History of Egypt">Egyptian civilization</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Sudan" title="History of Sudan">Sudanese kingdoms</a> have depended on the river and its annual flooding since ancient times. Most of the population and cities of Egypt lie along those parts of the Nile valley north of <a href="/wiki/Aswan_Dam" title="Aswan Dam">Aswan Dam</a>. Nearly all the cultural and historical sites of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egypt</a> developed and are found along river banks. The Nile is, with the <a href="/wiki/Rh%C3%B4ne" title="Rhône">Rhône</a> and <a href="/wiki/Po_(river)" title="Po (river)">Po</a>, one of the three Mediterranean rivers with the largest <a href="/wiki/Discharge_(hydrology)" title="Discharge (hydrology)">water discharge</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Margat_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Margat-15">[13]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<p>The standard English names "White Nile" and "Blue Nile" refer to the river's source, derived from <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> names formerly applied to only the Sudanese stretches that meet at Khartoum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911695_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911695-16">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the ancient <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_language" title="Egyptian language">Egyptian language</a>, the Nile is called <i>Ḥ'pī</i> (Hapy) or <i>Iteru</i>, meaning "river". In <a href="/wiki/Coptic_language" title="Coptic language">Coptic</a>, the word <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E2%B2%93%E2%B2%81%E2%B2%A3%E2%B2%9F" title="wikt:ⲓⲁⲣⲟ">ⲫⲓⲁⲣⲟ</a>, pronounced <i>piaro</i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sahidic" title="Sahidic">Sahidic</a>) or <i>phiaro</i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bohairic" title="Bohairic">Bohairic</a>), means "the river" (lit. p(h).iar-o "the.canal-great"), and comes from the same ancient name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hillel_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hillel-17">[15]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Nobiin_language" title="Nobiin language">Nobiin</a> the river is called <i>Áman Dawū</i>, meaning "the great water".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-3">[2]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Luganda" title="Luganda">Luganda</a> the river is called <i>Kiira</i> or <i>Kiyira</i>. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Runyoro" title="Runyoro">Runyoro</a> it is called <i>Kihiira</i>. In <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Arabic" title="Egyptian Arabic">Egyptian Arabic</a>, the Nile is called <i>en-Nīl</i>, while in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_Arabic" title="Standard Arabic">Standard Arabic</a> it is called <i>an-Nīl</i>. In <a href="/wiki/Biblical_Hebrew" title="Biblical Hebrew">Biblical Hebrew</a>, it is <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1020748213">.mw-parser-output .script-hebrew,.mw-parser-output .script-Hebr{font-family:"SBL Hebrew","SBL BibLit","Taamey Ashkenaz","Taamey Frank CLM","Frank Ruehl CLM","Ezra SIL","Ezra SIL SR","Keter Aram Tsova","Taamey David CLM","Keter YG","Shofar","David CLM","Hadasim CLM","Simple CLM","Nachlieli",Cardo,Alef,"Noto Serif Hebrew","Noto Sans Hebrew","David Libre",David,"Times New Roman",Gisha,Arial,FreeSerif,FreeSans}</style><span class="script-hebrew" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 115%;">הַיְאוֹר</span>‎, <i>Ha-Ye'or</i> or <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020748213" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-hebrew" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 115%;">הַשִׁיחוֹר</span>‎, <i>Ha-Shiḥor</i>.
</p><p>The English name <i>Nile</i> and the Arabic names <i>en-Nîl</i> and <i>an-Nîl</i> both derive from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Nilus</i></span></i> and the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a> <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">Νεῖλος</span></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-OED-18">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911693_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911693-19">[17]</a></sup> Beyond that, however, the etymology is disputed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911693_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911693-19">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[18]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a> called the river <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">Αἴγυπτος</span></span>, <i>Aiguptos</i>, but in subsequent periods, Greek authors referred to its lower course as <i>Neilos</i>; this term became generalised for the entire river system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goedicke_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-goedicke-21">[19]</a></sup> Thus, the name may derive from Ancient Egyptian expression <i>n</i>ꜣ <i>r</i>ꜣ<i>w-ḥ</i>ꜣ<i>w(t)</i> (lit. 'the mouths of the front parts'), which referred specifically to the branches of the Nile transversing the Delta, and would have been pronounced <i>ni-lo-he</i> in the area around <a href="/wiki/Memphis,_Egypt" title="Memphis, Egypt">Memphis</a> in the 8th century BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goedicke_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-goedicke-21">[19]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hesiod" title="Hesiod">Hesiod</a> at his <i><a href="/wiki/Theogony" title="Theogony">Theogony</a></i> refers to <a href="/wiki/Nilus_(mythology)" title="Nilus (mythology)">Nilus</a> (Νεῖλος) as one of the <a href="/wiki/Potamoi" title="Potamoi">Potamoi</a> (river gods), son of <a href="/wiki/Oceanus" title="Oceanus">Oceanus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tethys_(mythology)" title="Tethys (mythology)">Tethys</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another derivation of <i>Nile</i> might be related to the term <i>Nil</i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sanskrit_language" title="Sanskrit language">Sanskrit</a>: <span lang="sa">नील</span>, <small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Sanskrit" title="Romanization of Sanskrit">romanized</a>: </small><span title="Sanskrit-language romanization"><i lang="sa-Latn"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/nila" title="wikt:nila">nila</a></i></span>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egyptian_Arabic_language" title="Egyptian Arabic language">Egyptian Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="arz">نيلة</span>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hillel_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hillel-17">[15]</a></sup> which refers to <i><a href="/wiki/Indigofera_tinctoria" title="Indigofera tinctoria">Indigofera tinctoria</a></i>, one of the original sources of <a href="/wiki/Indigo_dye" title="Indigo dye">indigo dye</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[21]</a></sup> Another may be <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nymphaea_caerulea" title="Nymphaea caerulea">Nymphaea caerulea</a></i>, known as "The Sacred Blue Lily of the Nile", which was found scattered over <a href="/wiki/Tutankhamun" title="Tutankhamun">Tutankhamun</a>'s corpse when it was excavated in 1922.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[22]</a></sup> Another possible etymology derives from the <a href="/wiki/Semitic_languages" title="Semitic languages">Semitic</a> term <i>Nahal</i>, meaning "river".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[23]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Proto-Berber_language" title="Proto-Berber language">Old Libyan</a> has the term <i>lilu</i>, meaning water (in modern Berber <i>ilel</i> ⵉⵍⴻⵍ means <i>sea</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Courses">Courses</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nile_basin_map.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2160" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="396" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Nile_basin_map.png/220px-Nile_basin_map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Nile_basin_map.png/330px-Nile_basin_map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Nile_basin_map.png/440px-Nile_basin_map.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nile_basin_map.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Nile's <a href="/wiki/Drainage_basin" title="Drainage basin">drainage basin</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[25]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>With a total length of about 6,650 km (4,130 mi)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-length_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-length-1">[a]</a></sup> between the region of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Victoria" title="Lake Victoria">Lake Victoria</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>, the Nile is among the longest rivers on Earth. The <a href="/wiki/Drainage_basin" title="Drainage basin">drainage basin</a> of the Nile covers 3,254,555 square kilometers (1,256,591 sq mi), about 10% of the area of Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[26]</a></sup> Compared to other major rivers, though, the Nile carries little water (5% of the <a href="/wiki/Congo_River" title="Congo River">Congo River</a>, for example).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[27]</a></sup> The Nile basin is complex, and because of this, the <a href="/wiki/Discharge_(hydrology)" title="Discharge (hydrology)">discharge</a> at any given point along the <a href="/wiki/Main_stem" title="Main stem">main stem</a> depends on many factors including weather, diversions, <a href="/wiki/Evaporation" title="Evaporation">evaporation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Evapotranspiration" title="Evapotranspiration">evapotranspiration</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Groundwater" title="Groundwater">groundwater</a> flow.
</p><p>Upstream from <a href="/wiki/Khartoum" title="Khartoum">Khartoum</a> (to the south), the river is known as the <a href="/wiki/White_Nile" title="White Nile">White Nile</a>, a term also used in a limited sense to describe the section between <a href="/wiki/Lake_No" title="Lake No">Lake No</a> and Khartoum. At Khartoum, the river is joined by the <a href="/wiki/Blue_Nile" title="Blue Nile">Blue Nile</a>. The White Nile starts in equatorial <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East Africa</a>, and the Blue Nile begins in Ethiopia. Both branches are on the western flanks of the <a href="/wiki/East_African_Rift" title="East African Rift">East African Rift</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sources">Sources</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Source of the Nile" redirects here. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Source_of_the_Nile_(board_game)" title="Source of the Nile (board game)">Source of the Nile (board game)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Source_of_the_Nile_Bridge" title="Source of the Nile Bridge">Source of the Nile Bridge</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Source_of_Nile,_Spring_at_Jinja,_Lake_Victoria.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1877" data-file-width="2448" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Source_of_Nile%2C_Spring_at_Jinja%2C_Lake_Victoria.jpg/220px-Source_of_Nile%2C_Spring_at_Jinja%2C_Lake_Victoria.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Source_of_Nile%2C_Spring_at_Jinja%2C_Lake_Victoria.jpg/330px-Source_of_Nile%2C_Spring_at_Jinja%2C_Lake_Victoria.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Source_of_Nile%2C_Spring_at_Jinja%2C_Lake_Victoria.jpg/440px-Source_of_Nile%2C_Spring_at_Jinja%2C_Lake_Victoria.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Source_of_Nile,_Spring_at_Jinja,_Lake_Victoria.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Spring at Lake Victoria</div></div></div>
<p>The source of the Blue Nile is <a href="/wiki/Lake_Tana" title="Lake Tana">Lake Tana</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vijverberg,_Sibbing,_Dejen_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vijverberg,_Sibbing,_Dejen-30">[28]</a></sup> in the <a href="/wiki/Gish_Abay" title="Gish Abay">Gish Abay</a> region<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[29]</a></sup> in the <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Highlands" title="Ethiopian Highlands">Ethiopian Highlands</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morbach_et_al_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Morbach_et_al-33">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>The source of the White Nile, even after centuries of exploration, remains in dispute. The most remote source that is indisputably a source for the White Nile is the <a href="/wiki/Kagera_River" title="Kagera River">Kagera River</a>; however, the Kagera has tributaries that are in contention for the farthest source of the White Nile. Two start in Burundi: the <a href="/wiki/Ruvyironza_River" title="Ruvyironza River">Ruvyironza River</a> (also known as the Luvironza) and the <a href="/wiki/Rurubu_River" title="Rurubu River">Rurubu River</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-core.ac.uk/Aziz_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-core.ac.uk/Aziz-34">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morbach_et_al_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Morbach_et_al-33">[31]</a></sup> In addition, in 2010, an exploration party in Rwanda<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[33]</a></sup> went to a place described as the source of the <a href="/wiki/Rukarara_River" title="Rukarara River">Rukarara</a> tributary,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[34]</a></sup> and by hacking a path up steep jungle-choked mountain slopes in the <a href="/wiki/Nyungwe_Forest" title="Nyungwe Forest">Nyungwe Forest</a> found (in the <a href="/wiki/Dry_season" title="Dry season">dry season</a>) an appreciable incoming surface flow for many kilometres upstream, and found a new source, giving the Nile a length of 6,758 km (4,199 mi).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_Uganda">In Uganda</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:White_Nile_Fishermen_(18156464842).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1893" data-file-width="2525" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/White_Nile_Fishermen_%2818156464842%29.jpg/220px-White_Nile_Fishermen_%2818156464842%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/White_Nile_Fishermen_%2818156464842%29.jpg/330px-White_Nile_Fishermen_%2818156464842%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/White_Nile_Fishermen_%2818156464842%29.jpg/440px-White_Nile_Fishermen_%2818156464842%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:White_Nile_Fishermen_(18156464842).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/White_Nile" title="White Nile">White Nile</a> in Uganda</div></div></div>
<p>The White Nile leaves <a href="/wiki/Lake_Victoria" title="Lake Victoria">Lake Victoria</a> at <a href="/wiki/Ripon_Falls" title="Ripon Falls">Ripon Falls</a> near <a href="/wiki/Jinja,_Uganda" title="Jinja, Uganda">Jinja, Uganda</a>, as the "Victoria Nile." It flows north for some 130 kilometers (81 mi) to <a href="/wiki/Lake_Kyoga" title="Lake Kyoga">Lake Kyoga</a>. The last part of the approximately 200 kilometers (120 mi) river section starts from the western shores of the lake and flows at first to the west until just south of <a href="/wiki/Masindi_Port" title="Masindi Port">Masindi Port</a>, where the river turns north, then makes a great half circle to the east and north to <a href="/wiki/Karuma_Falls" title="Karuma Falls">Karuma Falls</a>. For the remaining part, it flows westerly through the <a href="/wiki/Murchison_Falls" title="Murchison Falls">Murchison Falls</a> until it reaches the northern shores of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Albert_(Africa)" title="Lake Albert (Africa)">Lake Albert</a> where it forms a significant river delta. Lake Albert is on the border of the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">Democratic Republic of the Congo</a>, but the Nile is not a border river at this point. After leaving Lake Albert, the river continues north through Uganda and is known as the <a href="/wiki/White_Nile#Albert_Nile" title="White Nile">Albert Nile</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="In_South_Sudan">In South Sudan</span></h4>
<p>The White Nile flows into <a href="/wiki/South_Sudan" title="South Sudan">South Sudan</a> just south of <a href="/wiki/Nimule" title="Nimule">Nimule</a>, where it is known as the <i>Bahr al Jabal</i> ("Mountain River"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[35]</a></sup>). Just south of the town is the <a href="/wiki/Confluence" title="Confluence">confluence</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Achwa_River" title="Achwa River">Achwa River</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Bahr_el_Ghazal_River" title="Bahr el Ghazal River">Bahr al Ghazal</a>, 716 kilometers (445 mi) long, joins the Bahr al Jabal at a small lagoon called <a href="/wiki/Lake_No" title="Lake No">Lake No</a>, after which the Nile becomes known as the <i>Bahr al Abyad</i>, or the White Nile, from the whitish <a href="/wiki/Clay" title="Clay">clay</a> suspended in its waters. When the <a href="/wiki/Flooding_of_the_Nile" title="Flooding of the Nile">Nile floods</a> it leaves a rich silty deposit which fertilizes the soil. The Nile no longer floods in Egypt since the completion of the Aswan Dam in 1970. An <a href="/wiki/Anabranch" title="Anabranch">anabranch</a> river, the <a href="/wiki/Bahr_el_Zeraf" title="Bahr el Zeraf">Bahr el Zeraf</a>, flows out of the Nile's Bahr al Jabal section and rejoins the White Nile.
</p><p>The flow rate of the Bahr al Jabal at <a href="/wiki/Mongalla,_South_Sudan" title="Mongalla, South Sudan">Mongalla</a> is almost constant throughout the year and averages 1,048 m<sup>3</sup>/s (37,000 cu ft/s). After Mongalla, the Bahr Al Jabal enters the enormous swamps of the <a href="/wiki/Sudd" title="Sudd">Sudd</a> region. More than half of the Nile's water is lost in this swamp to <a href="/wiki/Evaporation" title="Evaporation">evaporation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transpiration" title="Transpiration">transpiration</a>. The average flow rate of the White Nile at the tails of the swamps is about 510 m<sup>3</sup>/s (18,000 cu ft/s). From here it meets with the <a href="/wiki/Sobat_River" title="Sobat River">Sobat River</a> at <a href="/wiki/Malakal" title="Malakal">Malakal</a>. On an annual basis, the White Nile upstream of Malakal contributes about 15% of the total outflow of the Nile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>The average flow of the White Nile at Lake Kawaki Malakal, just below the Sobat River, is 924 m<sup>3</sup>/s (32,600 cu ft/s); the peak flow is approximately 1,218 m<sup>3</sup>/s (43,000 cu ft/s) in October and minimum flow is about 609 m<sup>3</sup>/s (21,500 cu ft/s) in April. This fluctuation is caused by the substantial variation in the flow of the Sobat, which has a minimum flow of about 99 m<sup>3</sup>/s (3,500 cu ft/s) in March and a peak flow of over 680 m<sup>3</sup>/s (24,000 cu ft/s) in October.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[37]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Dry_season" title="Dry season">dry season</a> (January to June) the White Nile contributes between 70% and 90% of the total discharge from the Nile.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_the_Sudan">In the Sudan</span></h3>
<p>Below <a href="/wiki/Renk,_South_Sudan" title="Renk, South Sudan">Renk</a>, the White Nile enters Sudan, it flows north to Khartoum and meets the Blue Nile.
</p><p>The course of the Nile in Sudan is distinctive. It flows over <a href="/wiki/Cataracts_of_the_Nile" title="Cataracts of the Nile">six groups of cataracts</a>, from the sixth at <a href="/wiki/Sabaloka_Game_Reserve" title="Sabaloka Game Reserve">Sabaloka</a> just north of Khartoum northward to <a href="/wiki/Abu_Hamad" title="Abu Hamad">Abu Hamad</a>. The tectonic uplift of the <a href="/wiki/Nubian_Swell" title="Nubian Swell">Nubian Swell</a> diverts the river south-west for over 300 km, following the structure of the <a href="/wiki/Central_African_Shear_Zone" title="Central African Shear Zone">Central African Shear Zone</a> embracing the <a href="/wiki/Bayuda_Desert" title="Bayuda Desert">Bayuda Desert</a>. At <a href="/wiki/Al_Dabbah,_Sudan" title="Al Dabbah, Sudan">Al Dabbah</a> it resumes its northward course towards the first cataract at <a href="/wiki/Aswan" title="Aswan">Aswan</a> forming the 'S'-shaped Great Bend of the Nile<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[38]</a></sup> mentioned by <a href="/wiki/Eratosthenes" title="Eratosthenes">Eratosthenes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the north of Sudan, the river enters <a href="/wiki/Lake_Nasser" title="Lake Nasser">Lake Nasser</a> (known in Sudan as Lake Nubia), the larger part of which is in Egypt.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="In_Egypt">In Egypt</span></h4>
<p>Below the <a href="/wiki/Aswan_Dam" title="Aswan Dam">Aswan Dam</a>, at the northern limit of Lake Nasser, the Nile resumes its historic course. North of <a href="/wiki/Cairo" title="Cairo">Cairo</a>, the Nile splits into two branches (or distributaries) that feed the Mediterranean: the <a href="/wiki/Rosetta" title="Rosetta">Rosetta</a> Branch to the west and the <a href="/wiki/Damietta" title="Damietta">Damietta</a> to the east, forming the <a href="/wiki/Nile_Delta" title="Nile Delta">Nile Delta</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sediment_transport">Sediment transport</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nile_River_and_delta_from_orbit.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="550" data-file-width="700" decoding="async" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Nile_River_and_delta_from_orbit.jpg/220px-Nile_River_and_delta_from_orbit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Nile_River_and_delta_from_orbit.jpg/330px-Nile_River_and_delta_from_orbit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Nile_River_and_delta_from_orbit.jpg/440px-Nile_River_and_delta_from_orbit.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nile_River_and_delta_from_orbit.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Nile_Delta" title="Nile Delta">Nile Delta</a> from space</div></div></div>
<p>The annual sediment transport by the Nile in Egypt has been quantified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hani_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-hani-42">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>At <a href="/wiki/Aswan" title="Aswan">Aswan</a>: 0.14 million tonnes of <a href="/wiki/Suspended_load" title="Suspended load">suspended sediment</a> and an additional 28% of <a href="/wiki/Bed_load" title="Bed load">bedload</a></li>
<li>At <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beni_Sweif" title="Beni Sweif">Beni Sweif</a>: 0.5 million tonnes of suspended sediment and an additional 20% of bedload</li>
<li>At <a href="/wiki/Qena" title="Qena">Qena</a>: 0.27 million tonnes of suspended sediment and an additional 27% of bedload</li>
<li>At <a href="/wiki/Sohag" title="Sohag">Sohag</a>: 1.5 million tonnes of suspended sediment and an additional 13% of bedload</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tributaries">Tributaries</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Red_Nile">Red Nile</span></h3>
<p>Below the confluence with the Blue Nile the only major tributary is the <a href="/wiki/Atbarah_River" title="Atbarah River">Atbarah River</a>, also known as the Red Nile, roughly halfway to the sea, which originates in Ethiopia north of Lake Tana, and is around 800 kilometers (500 mi) long. The Atbarah flows only while there is rain in Ethiopia and dries very rapidly. During the dry period of January to June, it typically dries up north of Khartoum.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Blue_Nile">Blue Nile</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Blue_Nile" title="Blue Nile">Blue Nile</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ET_Bahir_Dar_asv2018-02_img17_Tis_Issat.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5246" data-file-width="7869" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/ET_Bahir_Dar_asv2018-02_img17_Tis_Issat.jpg/220px-ET_Bahir_Dar_asv2018-02_img17_Tis_Issat.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/ET_Bahir_Dar_asv2018-02_img17_Tis_Issat.jpg/330px-ET_Bahir_Dar_asv2018-02_img17_Tis_Issat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/ET_Bahir_Dar_asv2018-02_img17_Tis_Issat.jpg/440px-ET_Bahir_Dar_asv2018-02_img17_Tis_Issat.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ET_Bahir_Dar_asv2018-02_img17_Tis_Issat.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Blue_Nile_Falls" title="Blue Nile Falls">Blue Nile Falls</a> fed by <a href="/wiki/Lake_Tana" title="Lake Tana">Lake Tana</a> near the city of <a href="/wiki/Bahir_Dar" title="Bahir Dar">Bahir Dar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ISS036-E-011050.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="720" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/ISS036-E-011050.jpg/220px-ISS036-E-011050.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/ISS036-E-011050.jpg/330px-ISS036-E-011050.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/ISS036-E-011050.jpg/440px-ISS036-E-011050.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ISS036-E-011050.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Annotated view of the Nile and Red Sea, with a dust storm<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[41]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The Blue Nile (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amharic_language" title="Amharic language">Amharic</a>: <span lang="am">ዓባይ</span>, <i>ʿĀbay</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[43]</a></sup>) springs from Lake Tana in the Ethiopian Highlands. The Blue Nile flows about 1,400 kilometres to Khartoum, where the Blue Nile and White Nile join to form the Nile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[44]</a></sup> Ninety percent of the water and ninety-six percent of the transported sediment carried by the Nile<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[45]</a></sup> originates in Ethiopia, with fifty-nine percent of the water from the Blue Nile (the rest being from the <a href="/wiki/Tekez%C3%A9_River" title="Tekezé River">Tekezé</a>, Atbarah, <a href="/wiki/Sobat_River" title="Sobat River">Sobat</a>, and small tributaries). The erosion and transportation of silt only occurs during the Ethiopian <a href="/wiki/Wet_season" title="Wet season">rainy season</a> when rainfall is especially high on the <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Highlands" title="Ethiopian Highlands">Ethiopian Plateau</a>; the rest of the year, the great rivers draining Ethiopia into the Nile have a weaker flow. In harsh and arid seasons and droughts, the Blue Nile dries out completely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>The flow of the Blue Nile varies considerably over its yearly cycle and is the main contribution to the large natural variation of the Nile flow. During the dry season the natural discharge of the Blue Nile can be as low as 113 m<sup>3</sup>/s (4,000 cu ft/s), although upstream dams regulate the flow of the river. During the wet season, the peak flow of the Blue Nile often exceeds 5,663 m<sup>3</sup>/s (200,000 cu ft/s) in late August (a difference of a factor of 50).
</p><p>Before the placement of dams on the river the yearly discharge varied by a factor of 15 at Aswan. Peak flows of over 8,212 m<sup>3</sup>/s (290,000 cu ft/s) occurred during late August and early September, and minimum flows of about 552 m<sup>3</sup>/s (19,500 cu ft/s) occurred during late April and early May.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bahr_el_Ghazal_and_Sobat_River">Bahr el Ghazal and Sobat River</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Bahr_el_Ghazal_River" title="Bahr el Ghazal River">Bahr al Ghazal</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sobat_River" title="Sobat River">Sobat River</a> are the two most important tributaries of the White Nile in terms of discharge.
</p><p>The Bahr al Ghazal's drainage basin is the largest of any of the Nile's sub-basins, measuring 520,000 square kilometers (200,000 sq mi) in size, but it contributes a relatively small amount of water, about 2 m<sup>3</sup>/s (71 cu ft/s) annually, because tremendous volumes of water are lost in the Sudd wetlands.
</p><p>The Sobat River, which joins the Nile a short distance below Lake No, drains about half as much land, 225,000 km<sup>2</sup> (86,900 sq mi), but contributes 412 cubic meters per second (14,500 cu ft/s) annually to the Nile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[47]</a></sup> When in flood the Sobat carries a large amount of sediment, adding greatly to the White Nile's color.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Yellow_Nile">Yellow Nile</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nile_Map_Sudan.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="846" data-file-width="919" decoding="async" height="258" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Nile_Map_Sudan.png/280px-Nile_Map_Sudan.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Nile_Map_Sudan.png/420px-Nile_Map_Sudan.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Nile_Map_Sudan.png/560px-Nile_Map_Sudan.png 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nile_Map_Sudan.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of Nile tributaries in modern Sudan, showing the Yellow Nile</div></div></div>
<p>The Yellow Nile is a former tributary that connected the <a href="/wiki/Ouadda%C3%AF_highlands" title="Ouaddaï highlands">Ouaddaï highlands</a> of eastern <a href="/wiki/Chad" title="Chad">Chad</a> to the Nile River Valley c. 8000 to c. 1000 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[49]</a></sup> Its remains are known as the <a href="/wiki/Wadi_Howar" title="Wadi Howar">Wadi Howar</a>. The wadi passes through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gharb_Darfur" title="Gharb Darfur">Gharb Darfur</a> near the northern border with Chad and meets up with the Nile near the southern point of the Great Bend.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Sahara#Climate_history" title="Sahara">Sahara § Climate history</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Herodotus_world_map-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="476" data-file-width="788" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Herodotus_world_map-en.svg/220px-Herodotus_world_map-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Herodotus_world_map-en.svg/330px-Herodotus_world_map-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Herodotus_world_map-en.svg/440px-Herodotus_world_map-en.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Herodotus_world_map-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Reconstruction of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oikoumene" title="Oikoumene">Oikoumene</a> (inhabited world), an ancient map based on <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a>' description of the world, circa 450 BC</div></div></div>
<p>The Nile has been the lifeline of civilization in Egypt since the <a href="/wiki/Stone_Age" title="Stone Age">Stone Age</a>, with most of the population and all of the cities of Egypt developing along those parts of the Nile valley lying north of Aswan. However, the Nile used to run much more westerly through what is now Wadi Hamim and Wadi al Maqar in <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a> and flow into the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Sidra" title="Gulf of Sidra">Gulf of Sidra</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[50]</a></sup> As the sea level rose at the end of the <a href="/wiki/Last_Glacial_Period" title="Last Glacial Period">most recent ice age</a>, the stream which is now the northern Nile <a href="/wiki/Stream_capture" title="Stream capture">captured</a> the ancestral Nile near <a href="/wiki/Asyut" title="Asyut">Asyut</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[51]</a></sup> This change in climate also led to the current extents of the <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a> desert, around 3400 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Khufu_branch">Khufu branch</span></h3>
<p>The Giza Pyramid Complex originally overlooked a branch of the Nile that no longer exists. This branch was highest during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_Humid_Period" title="African Humid Period">African Humid Period</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Solis-Moreira_2022_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Solis-Moreira_2022-56">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_Niles">Ancient Niles</span></h3>
<p>The existing Nile has five earlier phases; i) the Upper Miocenian Eonile, of about 6 million years <a href="/wiki/Before_Present" title="Before Present">BP</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rushdi_Said_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rushdi_Said-57">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Said_3_x_3_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Said_3_x_3-9">[7]</a></sup> ii) the Upper Pliocenian Paleonile, commencing about 3.32  million years BP, and during the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a>, the Nile phases iii) Proto-Nile, commencing about 600,000 years BP, iv) Pre-Nile,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Said/chapter/10.1007_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Said/chapter/10.1007-58">[56]</a></sup> transitioning at about 400,000 years BP to the<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rushdi_Said_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rushdi_Said-57">[55]</a></sup> v) Neo-Nile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Said/chapter/10.1007_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-Said/chapter/10.1007-58">[56]</a></sup> Flowing north from the Ethiopian Highlands, <a href="/wiki/Satellite_imagery" title="Satellite imagery">satellite imagery</a> was used to identify dry watercourses in the desert to the west of the Nile. A canyon, now filled by surface drift, represents the <b>Eonile</b> that flowed during 23–5.3 million years before present. The Eonile transported <a href="/wiki/Clastic_rock" title="Clastic rock">clastic sediments</a> to the Mediterranean; several <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> fields have been discovered within these sediments.
</p><p>During the late-<a href="/wiki/Miocene" title="Miocene">Miocene</a> <a href="/wiki/Messinian_salinity_crisis" title="Messinian salinity crisis">Messinian salinity crisis</a>, when the Mediterranean Sea was a <a href="/wiki/Endorheic_basin" title="Endorheic basin">closed basin</a> and evaporated to the point of being empty or nearly so, the Nile cut its course down to the new base level until it was several hundred metres below world ocean level at Aswan and 2,400 m (7,900 ft) below Cairo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Warren2006_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Warren2006-59">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mahmoudi2008_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mahmoudi2008-60">[58]</a></sup> This created a very long and deep canyon which was filled with sediment after the Mediterranean was recreated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Embabi2018_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Embabi2018-61">[59]</a></sup> At some point the sediments raised the riverbed sufficiently for the river to overflow westward into a depression to create <a href="/wiki/Lake_Moeris" title="Lake Moeris">Lake Moeris</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Lake_Tanganyika" title="Lake Tanganyika">Lake Tanganyika</a> drained northwards into the Nile until the <a href="/wiki/Virunga_Mountains" title="Virunga Mountains">Virunga Volcanoes</a> blocked its course in Rwanda. The Nile was much longer at that time, with its furthest headwaters in northern <a href="/wiki/Zambia" title="Zambia">Zambia</a>. The currently existing Nile first flowed during the former parts of the <a href="/wiki/W%C3%BCrm_glaciation" title="Würm glaciation">Würm glaciation</a> period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Said_3_x_3_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-Said_3_x_3-9">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Integrated_Nile">Integrated Nile</span></h3>
<p>There are two theories about the age of the integrated Nile. One is that the integrated drainage of the Nile is of young age and that the Nile basin was formerly broken into series of separate basins, only the most northerly of which fed a river following the present course of the Nile in Egypt and Sudan. Rushdi Said postulates that Egypt supplied most of the waters of the Nile during the early part of its history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Said1981_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Said1981-62">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>The other theory is that the drainage from Ethiopia via rivers equivalent to the Blue Nile, the Atbara and the Takazze flowed to the Mediterranean via the Egyptian Nile since well back into <a href="/wiki/Tertiary" title="Tertiary">Tertiary</a> times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WW1980_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-WW1980-63">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>Salama suggests that during the <a href="/wiki/Paleogene" title="Paleogene">Paleogene</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neogene" title="Neogene">Neogene</a> Periods (66 million to 2.588 million years ago) a series of separate closed continental basins each occupied one of the major parts of the Sudanese Rift System: <a href="/wiki/Melut_Basin" title="Melut Basin">Mellut rift</a>, <a href="/wiki/White_Nile_rift" title="White Nile rift">White Nile rift</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blue_Nile_rift" title="Blue Nile rift">Blue Nile rift</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Atbara_rift&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Atbara rift (page does not exist)">Atbara rift</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Sag_El_Naam_rift&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Sag El Naam rift (page does not exist)">Sag El Naam rift</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Salama1987_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Salama1987-64">[62]</a></sup>
The Mellut Basin is nearly 12 kilometers (7.5 mi) deep at its central part. This rift is possibly still active, with reported <a href="/wiki/Tectonics" title="Tectonics">tectonic</a> activity in its northern and southern boundaries. The <a href="/wiki/Sudd" title="Sudd">Sudd</a> swamp which forms the central part of the basin may still be subsiding. The White Nile Rift system, although shallower than the <a href="/wiki/Bahr_el_Arab_rift" title="Bahr el Arab rift">Bahr el Arab rift</a>, is about 9 kilometers (5.6 mi) deep. Geophysical exploration of the Blue Nile Rift System estimated the depth of the sediments to be 5–9 kilometers (3.1–5.6 mi). These basins were not interconnected until their subsidence ceased, and the rate of sediment deposition was enough to fill and connect them. 
</p><p>The Egyptian Nile connected to the Sudanese Nile, which captures the Ethiopian and Equatorial headwaters during the current stages of tectonic activity in the Eastern, Central and Sudanese Rift systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Salama1997_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Salama1997-65">[63]</a></sup> The connection of the different Niles occurred during cyclic wet periods. The Atbarah overflowed its closed basin during the wet periods that occurred about 100,000 to 120,000 years ago. The Blue Nile connected to the main Nile during the 70,000–80,000 years B.P. wet period. The White Nile system in Bahr El Arab and White Nile Rifts remained a closed lake until the connection of the Victoria Nile to the main system some 12,500 years ago during the <a href="/wiki/African_humid_period" title="African humid period">African humid period</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_in_the_founding_of_Egyptian_civilization">Role in the founding of Egyptian civilization</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_agriculture" title="Ancient Egyptian agriculture">Ancient Egyptian agriculture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vallee_fertile_du_Nil_a_Louxor.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1368" data-file-width="2080" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Vallee_fertile_du_Nil_a_Louxor.jpg/220px-Vallee_fertile_du_Nil_a_Louxor.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Vallee_fertile_du_Nil_a_Louxor.jpg/330px-Vallee_fertile_du_Nil_a_Louxor.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Vallee_fertile_du_Nil_a_Louxor.jpg/440px-Vallee_fertile_du_Nil_a_Louxor.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vallee_fertile_du_Nil_a_Louxor.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An aerial view of <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">irrigation</a> from the Nile River supporting agriculture in <a href="/wiki/Luxor" title="Luxor">Luxor, Egypt</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dhows_on_the_Nile.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2595" data-file-width="1817" decoding="async" height="243" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Dhows_on_the_Nile.jpg/170px-Dhows_on_the_Nile.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Dhows_on_the_Nile.jpg/255px-Dhows_on_the_Nile.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Dhows_on_the_Nile.jpg/340px-Dhows_on_the_Nile.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dhows_on_the_Nile.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Felucca" title="Felucca">felucca</a> traversing the Nile near Aswan</div></div></div>
<p>The Greek historian <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> wrote that "Egypt was the gift of the Nile". An unending source of sustenance, it played a crucial role in the development of Egyptian civilization. Because the river overflowed its banks annually and deposited new layers of silt, the surrounding land was very fertile. The <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egyptians</a> cultivated and traded <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flax" title="Flax">flax</a>, <a href="/wiki/Papyrus" title="Papyrus">papyrus</a> and other crops around the Nile. Wheat was a crucial crop in the famine-plagued <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>. This trading system secured Egypt's diplomatic relationships with other countries and contributed to economic stability. Far-reaching trade has been carried on along the Nile since ancient times. A tune, <a href="/wiki/Hymn_to_the_Nile" title="Hymn to the Nile">Hymn to the Nile</a>, was created and sung by the ancient Egyptian peoples about the flooding of the Nile River and all of the miracles it brought to Ancient Egyptian civilization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-66">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Water_buffalo" title="Water buffalo">Water buffalo</a> were introduced from Asia, and the <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">Assyrians</a> introduced <a href="/wiki/Camel" title="Camel">camels</a> in the 7th century BC. These animals were raised for meat and were domesticated and used for ploughing—or in the camels' case, carriage. Water was vital to both people and livestock. The Nile was also a convenient and efficient means of transportation for people and goods.
</p><p>The Nile was also an important part of ancient Egyptian spiritual life. <a href="/wiki/Hapi_(Nile_god)" title="Hapi (Nile god)">Hapi</a> was the god of the annual floods, and both he and the <a href="/wiki/Pharaoh" title="Pharaoh">pharaoh</a> were thought to control the flooding. The Nile was considered to be a causeway from life to death and the afterlife. The east was thought of as a place of birth and growth, and the west was considered the place of death, as the god <a href="/wiki/Ra" title="Ra">Ra</a>, the Sun, underwent birth, death, and resurrection each day as he crossed the sky. Thus, all tombs were west of the Nile, because the Egyptians believed that in order to enter the afterlife, they had to be buried on the side that symbolized death.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>As the Nile was such an important factor in Egyptian life, the ancient calendar was even based on the three cycles of the Nile. These seasons, each consisting of four months of thirty days each, were called <a href="/wiki/Season_of_the_Inundation" title="Season of the Inundation">Akhet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Season_of_the_Emergence" title="Season of the Emergence">Peret</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Season_of_the_Harvest" title="Season of the Harvest">Shemu</a>. Akhet, which means inundation, was the time of the year when the Nile flooded, leaving several layers of fertile soil behind, aiding in agricultural growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SpringerMorris2010_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-SpringerMorris2010-67">[65]</a></sup> Peret was the growing season, and Shemu, the last season, was the harvest season when there were no rains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SpringerMorris2010_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-SpringerMorris2010-67">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="European_search_for_the_source">European search for the source</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:JH_Speke.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="772" data-file-width="604" decoding="async" height="217" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/JH_Speke.jpg/170px-JH_Speke.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/JH_Speke.jpg/255px-JH_Speke.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/JH_Speke.jpg/340px-JH_Speke.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:JH_Speke.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/John_Hanning_Speke" title="John Hanning Speke">John Hanning Speke</a> <span title="circa">c.</span><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1863</span>. Speke was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Victorian_Age" title="Victorian Age">Victorian</a> explorer who first reached <a href="/wiki/Lake_Victoria" title="Lake Victoria">Lake Victoria</a> in 1858, returning to establish it as the source of the Nile by 1862.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911698_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911698-68">[66]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Owing to their failure to penetrate the Sudd wetlands of South Sudan, the upper reaches of the White Nile remained largely unknown to the ancient Greeks and Romans. Various expeditions failed to determine the river's source. <a href="/wiki/Agatharchides" title="Agatharchides">Agatharchides</a> records that in the time of <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy_II_Philadelphus" title="Ptolemy II Philadelphus">Ptolemy II Philadelphus</a>, a military expedition had penetrated far enough along the course of the Blue Nile to determine that the summer floods were caused by heavy seasonal rainstorms in the Ethiopian Highlands, but no European of antiquity is known to have reached Lake Tana. The <i><a href="/wiki/Tabula_Rogeriana" title="Tabula Rogeriana">Tabula Rogeriana</a></i> depicted the source as three lakes in 1154.
</p><p>Europeans began to learn about the origins of the Nile in the 14th century when the Pope sent monks as emissaries to <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a> who passed India, the Middle East and Africa, and described being told of the source of the Nile in Abyssinia (Ethiopia)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-THE_TRAVELS_OF_JOHN_DE_MARIGNOLLI_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-THE_TRAVELS_OF_JOHN_DE_MARIGNOLLI-69">[67]</a></sup> Later in the 15th and 16th centuries, travelers to Ethiopia visited Lake Tana and the source of the Blue Nile in the mountains south of the lake. Although <a href="/wiki/James_Bruce" title="James Bruce">James Bruce</a> claimed to be the first European to have visited the headwaters,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[68]</a></sup> modern writers give the credit to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Society_of_Jesus" title="Society of Jesus">Jesuit</a> <a href="/wiki/Pedro_P%C3%A1ez" title="Pedro Páez">Pedro Páez</a>. Páez's account of the source of the Nile<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[69]</a></sup> is a long and vivid account of Ethiopia. It was published in full only in the early 20th century, although it was featured in works of Páez's contemporaries, including Baltazar Téllez,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[70]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Athanasius_Kircher" title="Athanasius Kircher">Athanasius Kircher</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[71]</a></sup> and by <a href="/wiki/Johann_Michael_Vansleb" title="Johann Michael Vansleb">Johann Michael Vansleb</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>Europeans had been resident in Ethiopia since the late 15th century, and one of them may have visited the headwaters even earlier without leaving a written trace. The Portuguese João Bermudes published the first description of the <a href="/wiki/Blue_Nile_Falls" title="Blue Nile Falls">Tis Issat</a> Falls in his 1565 memoirs, compared them to the Nile Falls alluded to in <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a>'s <i>De Republica</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[73]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jer%C3%B3nimo_Lobo" title="Jerónimo Lobo">Jerónimo Lobo</a> describes the source of the Blue Nile, visiting shortly after Pedro Páez. Telles also uses his account.
</p><p>The White Nile was even less understood. The ancients mistakenly believed that the <a href="/wiki/Niger_River" title="Niger River">Niger River</a> represented the upper reaches of the White Nile. For example, <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a> writes that the Nile had its origins "in a mountain of lower <a href="/wiki/Mauretania" title="Mauretania">Mauretania</a>", flowed above ground for "many days" distance, then went underground, reappeared as a large lake in the territories of the <a href="/wiki/Masaesyli" title="Masaesyli">Masaesyli</a>, then sank again below the desert to flow underground "for a distance of 20 days' journey till it reaches the nearest Ethiopians."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1911Nile.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1310" data-file-width="652" decoding="async" height="342" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/1911Nile.png/170px-1911Nile.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/1911Nile.png/255px-1911Nile.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/1911Nile.png/340px-1911Nile.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1911Nile.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A map of the Nile <span title="circa">c.</span><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1911</span>, a time when its entire primary course ran through British occupations, condominiums, colonies, and protectorates<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911693_19-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911693-19">[17]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Modern exploration of the Nile basin began with the <a href="/wiki/Turco-Egyptian_conquest_of_Sudan_(1820%E2%80%931824)" title="Turco-Egyptian conquest of Sudan (1820–1824)">conquest of the northern and central Sudan</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a> viceroy of Egypt, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali_of_Egypt" title="Muhammad Ali of Egypt">Muhammad Ali</a>, and his sons from 1821 onward. As a result of this, the Blue Nile was known as far as its exit from the Ethiopian foothills and the White Nile as far as the mouth of the Sobat River. Three expeditions under a Turkish officer, Selim Bimbashi, were made between 1839 and 1842, and two got to the point about 30 kilometres (20 miles) beyond the present port of <a href="/wiki/Juba" title="Juba">Juba</a>, where the country rises and rapids make navigation very difficult.
</p><p>Lake Victoria was first sighted by Europeans in 1858 when British explorer <a href="/wiki/John_Hanning_Speke" title="John Hanning Speke">John Hanning Speke</a> reached its southern shore while traveling with <a href="/wiki/Richard_Francis_Burton" title="Richard Francis Burton">Richard Francis Burton</a> to explore central Africa and locate the great lakes. Believing he had found the source of the Nile on seeing this "vast expanse of open water" for the first time, Speke named the lake after <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">Queen Victoria</a>. Burton, recovering from illness and resting further south on the shores of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Tanganyika" title="Lake Tanganyika">Lake Tanganyika</a>, was outraged that Speke claimed to have proven his discovery to be the true source of the Nile when Burton regarded this as still unsettled. A quarrel ensued which sparked intense debate within the scientific community and interest by other explorers keen to either confirm or refute Speke's discovery. British explorer and missionary <a href="/wiki/David_Livingstone" title="David Livingstone">David Livingstone</a> pushed too far west and entered the <a href="/wiki/Congo_River" title="Congo River">Congo River</a> system instead. It was ultimately Welsh-American explorer <a href="/wiki/Henry_Morton_Stanley" title="Henry Morton Stanley">Henry Morton Stanley</a> who confirmed Speke's discovery, circumnavigating Lake Victoria and reporting the great outflow at <a href="/wiki/Ripon_Falls" title="Ripon Falls">Ripon Falls</a> on the lake's northern shore.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Cammell_Laird" title="Cammell Laird">Laird Shipyard</a> of <a href="/wiki/Liverpool" title="Liverpool">Liverpool</a> sent an iron steamer to the Nile in the 1830s. With the completion of the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal" title="Suez Canal">Suez Canal</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Egyptian_War" title="Anglo-Egyptian War">British takeover of Egypt</a> in 1882, more British river steamers followed. The Nile is the area's natural navigation channel, giving access to Khartoum and Sudan by steamer. The <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Khartoum" title="Siege of Khartoum">Siege of Khartoum</a> was broken with purpose-built <a href="/wiki/Paddle_steamer" title="Paddle steamer">sternwheelers</a> shipped from England and steamed up the river to retake the city. After this came regular steam navigation of the river. 
</p><p>With <a href="/wiki/History_of_Egypt_under_the_British" title="History of Egypt under the British">British presence in Egypt</a> in <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> and the inter-war years, river steamers provided both security and sightseeing to the <a href="/wiki/Giza_pyramid_complex" title="Giza pyramid complex">pyramids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thebes,_Egypt" title="Thebes, Egypt">Thebes</a>. Steam navigation remained integral to the two countries as late as 1962. Sudan steamer traffic was a lifeline as few railways or roads were built in that country. Most paddle steamers have been retired to shorefront service, but modern diesel tourist boats remain on the river.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Since_1950">Since 1950</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:KageraRuvubu.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/KageraRuvubu.jpg/220px-KageraRuvubu.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/KageraRuvubu.jpg/330px-KageraRuvubu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/KageraRuvubu.jpg/440px-KageraRuvubu.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:KageraRuvubu.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The confluence of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kagera_river" title="Kagera river">Kagera</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ruvubu_river" title="Ruvubu river">Ruvubu</a> rivers near <a href="/wiki/Rusumo_Falls" title="Rusumo Falls">Rusumo Falls</a>, part of the Nile's upper reaches</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nile03(js).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1276" data-file-width="1920" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Nile03%28js%29.jpg/220px-Nile03%28js%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Nile03%28js%29.jpg/330px-Nile03%28js%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Nile03%28js%29.jpg/440px-Nile03%28js%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nile03(js).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dhows" title="Dhows">Dhows</a> on the Nile</div></div></div> <div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:View_from_Cairo_Tower_31march2007.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1331" data-file-width="1718" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/View_from_Cairo_Tower_31march2007.jpg/220px-View_from_Cairo_Tower_31march2007.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/View_from_Cairo_Tower_31march2007.jpg/330px-View_from_Cairo_Tower_31march2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/View_from_Cairo_Tower_31march2007.jpg/440px-View_from_Cairo_Tower_31march2007.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:View_from_Cairo_Tower_31march2007.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Nile passes through Cairo, Egypt's capital city.</div></div></div>
<p>The Nile has long been used to transport goods along its length. Winter winds blow south, up river, so ships could sail up river using sails and down river using the flow of the river. While most Egyptians still live in the Nile valley, the 1970 completion of the Aswan Dam ended the summer floods and their renewal of the fertile soil, fundamentally changing farming practices. The Nile supports much of the population living along its banks, enabling Egyptians to live in otherwise inhospitable regions of the Sahara. The river's flow is disturbed at several points by the Cataracts of the Nile which form an obstacle to navigation by boats. The Sudd also forms a formidable navigation obstacle and impedes water flow, to the extent that Sudan had once attempted to build the <a href="/wiki/Jonglei_Canal" title="Jonglei Canal">Jonglei Canal</a> to bypass the swamp.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nile cities include Khartoum, Aswan, <a href="/wiki/Luxor" title="Luxor">Luxor</a> (Thebes), and the <a href="/wiki/Giza" title="Giza">Giza</a> – Cairo <a href="/wiki/Conurbation" title="Conurbation">conurbation</a>. The first cataract, the closest to the mouth of the river, is at Aswan, north of the Aswan Dam. This part of the river is a regular tourist route, with cruise ships and traditional wooden sailing boats known as <a href="/wiki/Felucca" title="Felucca">feluccas</a>. Many cruise ships ply the route between Luxor and Aswan, stopping at <a href="/wiki/Edfu" title="Edfu">Edfu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kom_Ombo" title="Kom Ombo">Kom Ombo</a> along the way. Security concerns have limited cruising on the northernmost portion for many years.
</p><p>A computer simulation study to plan the economic development of the Nile was directed by H.A.W. Morrice and W.N. Allan, for the Ministry of Hydro-power of Sudan, during 1955–57<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[79]</a></sup> Morrice was their hydrological adviser, and Allan his predecessor. The calculations were enabled by accurate monthly inflow data collected for 50 years. The underlying principle was the use of over-year storage, to conserve water from rainy years for use in dry years. Irrigation, navigation and other needs were considered. Each computer run postulated a set of reservoirs and operating equations for the release of water as a function of the month and the levels upstream. The behavior that would have resulted given the inflow data was modeled. Over 600 models were run. Recommendations were made to the Sudanese authorities. The calculations were run on an IBM 650 computer. Simulation studies to design water resources are discussed further in the article on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrology_transport_model" title="Hydrology transport model">hydrology transport models</a>, which have been used since the 1980s to analyze water quality.
</p><p>Despite the development of many reservoirs, drought during the 1980s led to widespread starvation in Ethiopia and Sudan, but Egypt was nourished by water impounded in Lake Nasser. Drought has proven to be a major cause of fatality in the Nile river basin. According to a report by the Strategic Foresight Group around 170 million people have been affected by droughts in the last century with half a million lives lost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blue_Peace_for_the_Nile,_2009_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blue_Peace_for_the_Nile,_2009-82">[80]</a></sup> From the 70 incidents of drought which took place between 1900 and 2012, 55 incidents took place in Ethiopia, Sudan, South Sudan, Kenya and Tanzania.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blue_Peace_for_the_Nile,_2009_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-Blue_Peace_for_the_Nile,_2009-82">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_sharing_dispute">Water sharing dispute</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nile_hydro_power.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1724" data-file-width="1302" decoding="async" height="291" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Nile_hydro_power.jpg/220px-Nile_hydro_power.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Nile_hydro_power.jpg/330px-Nile_hydro_power.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Nile_hydro_power.jpg/440px-Nile_hydro_power.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nile_hydro_power.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hydropower dams in the Nile (plus huge dam under construction in Ethiopia)</div></div></div>
<p>The Nile's water has affected the politics of East Africa and the <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a> for many decades. The dispute between Egypt and Ethiopia over the $4.5 billion <a href="/wiki/Grand_Ethiopian_Renaissance_Dam" title="Grand Ethiopian Renaissance Dam">Grand Ethiopian Renaissance Dam</a> has become a national preoccupation in both countries, stoking patriotism, deep-seated fears and even murmurs of war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[81]</a></sup> Countries including Uganda, Sudan, Ethiopia and Kenya have complained about Egyptian domination of its water resources. The <a href="/wiki/Nile_Basin_Initiative" title="Nile Basin Initiative">Nile Basin Initiative</a> promotes a peaceful cooperation among those states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several attempts have been made to establish agreements between the countries sharing the Nile waters. On 14 May 2010 at <a href="/wiki/Entebbe" title="Entebbe">Entebbe</a>, Uganda, Ethiopia, Rwanda, and Tanzania signed a new agreement on sharing the Nile water even though this agreement raised strong opposition from Egypt and Sudan. Ideally, such international agreements should promote equitable and efficient usage of the Nile basin's water resources. Without a better understanding about the availability of the future water resources of the Nile, it is possible that conflicts could arise between these countries relying on the Nile for their water supply, economic and social developments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hdl.handle_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-hdl.handle-12">[10]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_achievements_and_exploration">Modern achievements and exploration</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="White_Nile">White Nile</span></h3>
<p>In 1951, American <a href="/wiki/John_Goddard_(adventurer)" title="John Goddard (adventurer)">John Goddard</a> together with two French explorers became the first to successfully navigate the entire Nile from its source in Burundi at the potential headsprings of the Kagera River in Burundi to its mouth on the Mediterranean Sea, a journey of approximately 6,800 km (4,200 mi). Their 9-month journey is described in the book <i>Kayaks down the Nile</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>The White Nile Expedition, led by <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South African</a> national <a href="/wiki/Hendrik_Coetzee" title="Hendrik Coetzee">Hendrik Coetzee</a>, navigated the White Nile's entire length of approximately 3,700 kilometres (2,300 mi). The expedition began at the White Nile's beginning at Lake Victoria in Uganda, on 17 January 2004 and arrived at the Mediterranean in Rosetta, four and a half months later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Blue_Nile_2">Blue Nile</span></h3>
<p>The Blue Nile Expedition, led by geologist Pasquale Scaturro and his partner, kayaker and documentary filmmaker Gordon Brown became the first known people to descend the entire Blue Nile, from Lake Tana in Ethiopia to the beaches of Alexandria on the Mediterranean. Their approximately 5,230-kilometre (3,250 mi) journey took 114 days, from 25 December 2003 to 28 April 2004. Though their expedition included others, Brown and Scaturro were the only ones to complete the entire journey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[86]</a></sup> Although they descended <a href="/wiki/Whitewater" title="Whitewater">whitewater</a> manually, the team used <a href="/wiki/Outboard_motor" title="Outboard motor">outboard motors</a> for much of their journey.
</p><p>On 29 January 2005, Canadian Les Jickling and New Zealander Mark Tanner completed the first human-powered transit of Ethiopia's Blue Nile. Their journey of over 5,000 kilometres (3,100 mi) took five months. They recount that they paddled through two war zones, regions notorious for bandits, and were arrested at gunpoint.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Crossings">Crossings</span></h2>
