</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kasr_Al_Nile_Bridge.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3216" data-file-width="4288" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Kasr_Al_Nile_Bridge.JPG/220px-Kasr_Al_Nile_Bridge.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Kasr_Al_Nile_Bridge.JPG/330px-Kasr_Al_Nile_Bridge.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Kasr_Al_Nile_Bridge.JPG/440px-Kasr_Al_Nile_Bridge.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kasr_Al_Nile_Bridge.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>View of the Qasr El Nil Bridge in Cairo, with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gezira_Island" title="Gezira Island">Gezira Island</a> in the background</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Khartoum,_Sudan_(32485465841)2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2052" data-file-width="2488" decoding="async" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Khartoum%2C_Sudan_%2832485465841%292.jpg/220px-Khartoum%2C_Sudan_%2832485465841%292.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Khartoum%2C_Sudan_%2832485465841%292.jpg/330px-Khartoum%2C_Sudan_%2832485465841%292.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Khartoum%2C_Sudan_%2832485465841%292.jpg/440px-Khartoum%2C_Sudan_%2832485465841%292.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Khartoum,_Sudan_(32485465841)2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/El_Mek_Nimr_Bridge" title="El Mek Nimr Bridge">El Mek Nimr Bridge</a> in Khartoum</div></div></div>

<h3><span id="Crossings_from_Jinja.2C_Uganda_to_Khartoum"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Crossings_from_Jinja,_Uganda_to_Khartoum">Crossings from Jinja, Uganda to Khartoum</span></h3>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Source_of_the_Nile_Bridge" title="Source of the Nile Bridge">Source of the Nile Bridge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jinja_District" title="Jinja District">Jinja</a>, Uganda</li>
<li>River Nile Railway Bridge, <a href="/wiki/Jinja_District" title="Jinja District">Jinja</a>, Uganda</li>
<li>Nalubaale Bridge, <a href="/wiki/Jinja,_Uganda" title="Jinja, Uganda">Jinja, Uganda</a> (Formerly <a href="/wiki/Owen_Falls" title="Owen Falls">Owen Falls Bridge</a>)</li>
<li>Karuma Bridge, <a href="/wiki/Karuma" title="Karuma">Karuma</a>, Uganda</li>
<li>Pakwach Bridge, Uganda</li></ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Annotated_bibliography">Annotated bibliography</span></h2>
<p>The following is an annotated bibliography of key written documents for the Western exploration of the Nile.
</p><p><b>17th century</b>
</p>
<ul><li><i>Historia da Ethiopia</i>, <a href="/wiki/Pedro_P%C3%A1ez" title="Pedro Páez">Pedro Páez</a> (aka Pero Pais), Portugal, 1620</li></ul>
<dl><dd>A Jesuit missionary who was sent from <a href="/wiki/Goa" title="Goa">Goa</a> to <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> in 1589 and remained in the area until his death in 1622. Credited with being the first European to view the source of the Blue Nile which he describes in this volume.</dd></dl>
<ul><li><i>Voyage historique d'Abissinie</i>, <a href="/wiki/Jer%C3%B3nimo_Lobo" title="Jerónimo Lobo">Jerónimo Lobo</a> (aka Girolamo Lobo), Piero Matini, Firenze; 1693</li></ul>
<dl><dd>One of the most important and earliest sources on Ethiopia and the Nile. Jerónimo Lobo (1595–1687), a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesuit" title="Jesuit">Jesuit</a> priest, stayed in Ethiopia, mostly in <a href="/wiki/Tigray_Province" title="Tigray Province">Tigre</a>, for 9 years and travelled to <a href="/wiki/Lake_Tana" title="Lake Tana">Lake Tana</a> and the Blue Nile, reaching the province of <a href="/wiki/Danot_(woreda)" title="Danot (woreda)">Damot</a>. When the Jesuits were expelled from the country, he too had to leave and did so via <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Massaua" title="Massaua">Massaua</a> and <a href="/wiki/Suakin" title="Suakin">Suakin</a>. "He was the best expert on Ethiopian matters. After Pais, Lobo is the second European to describe the sources of the Blue Nile and he did so more exactly than Bruce" (transl. from Henze).</dd></dl>
<p><b>18th century</b>
</p>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Travels_to_Discover_the_Source_of_the_Nile" title="Travels to Discover the Source of the Nile">Travels to Discover the Source of the Nile in the Years – 1768, 1770, 1771, 1772, and 1773</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/James_Bruce" title="James Bruce">James Bruce</a> of Kinnaird. J. Ruthven for G. GJ. and J. Robinson et al., Edinburgh, 1790 (5 Volumes)</li></ul>
<dl><dd>With time on his hands and at the urging of a friend, Bruce composed this account of his travels on the African continent, including comments on the history and religion of Egypt, an account of Indian trade, a history of <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Abyssinia</a>, and other material. Although Bruce would not be confused with "a great scholar or a judicious critic, few books of equal compass are equally entertaining; and few such monuments exist of the energy and enterprise of a single traveller" (DNB). "The result of his travels was a very great enrichment of the knowledge of geography and ethnography" (Cox II, p. 389.) Bruce was one of the earliest westerners to search for the source of the Nile. In November 1770 he reached the source of the Blue Nile, and though he acknowledged that the White Nile was the larger stream, he claimed that the Blue Nile was the Nile of the ancients and that he was thus the discoverer of its source. The account of his travels was written twelve years after his journey and without reference to his journals, which gave critics grounds for disbelief, but the substantial accuracy of the book has since been amply demonstrated.</dd></dl>
<p><b>1800–1850</b>
</p>
<ul><li><i>Egypt And Mohammed Ali, Or Travels in the Valley of The Nile</i>, <a href="/wiki/James_Augustus_St._John" title="James Augustus St. John">James Augustus St. John</a>, Longman, London, 1834</li></ul>
<dl><dd>St. John traveled extensively in Egypt and <a href="/wiki/Nubia" title="Nubia">Nubia</a> in 1832–33, mainly on foot. He gives a very interesting picture of Egyptian life and politics under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali_of_Egypt" title="Muhammad Ali of Egypt">Mohammed Ali</a>; a large part of volume II deals with the Egyptian campaign in <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>.</dd></dl>
<ul><li><i>Travels in Ethiopia Above the Second Cateract of the Nile; Exhibiting the State of That Country and Its Various Inhabitants Under the Dominion of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali_of_Egypt" title="Muhammad Ali of Egypt">Mohammed Ali</a>; and Illustrating the Antiquities, Arts, and History of the Ancient Kingdom of Meroe</i>, G.A. Hoskins. Longman, Rees, Orme, Brown, Green, &amp; Longman, London; 1835.</li>
<li><i>Modern Egypt and Thebes: Being a Description of Egypt; Including Information Required for Travelers in That Country</i>, Sir Gardner Wilkinson, John Murray, London, 1843</li></ul>
<dl><dd>The first known English travelers guide to the Lower Nile Basin.</dd></dl>
<p><b>1850–1900</b>
</p>
<ul><li><i>Lake Regions of Central Equatorial Africa, with Notices of The Lunar Mountains and the Sources of the White Nile; being The Results of an Expedition Undertaken under the Patronage of Her Majesty's Government and the Royal Geographical Society of London, In the Years 1857–1859</i>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Richard_Burton" title="Sir Richard Burton">Sir Richard Burton</a>. <a href="/wiki/William_Clowes_Ltd." title="William Clowes Ltd.">W. Clowes</a>, London; 1860</li></ul>
<dl><dd>Sir Richard Burton's presentation of his expedition with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Speke" title="John Speke">John Speke</a>. Ultimately, Burton's view of the sources of the Nile failed and Speke's prevailed.</dd></dl>
<ul><li><i>Travels, researches, and missionary labours, during eighteen years' residence in eastern Africa. Together with journeys to Jagga, Usambara, Ukambani, Shoa, Abessinia, and Khartum; and a coasting voyage from Mombaz to Cape Delgado. With an appendix respecting the snow-capped mountains of eastern Africa; the sources of the Nile; the languages and literature of Abessinia And eastern Africa, etc. etc.</i>, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Ludwig_Krapf" title="Johann Ludwig Krapf">Rev Dr. J. Krapf</a>, Trubner and Co, London; 1860; <a href="/wiki/Ticknor_and_Fields" title="Ticknor and Fields">Ticknor and Fields</a>, Boston; 1860</li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Henry_Morton_Stanley_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1433" data-file-width="911" decoding="async" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Henry_Morton_Stanley_1.jpg/170px-Henry_Morton_Stanley_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Henry_Morton_Stanley_1.jpg/255px-Henry_Morton_Stanley_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Henry_Morton_Stanley_1.jpg/340px-Henry_Morton_Stanley_1.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Henry_Morton_Stanley_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Henry_Morton_Stanley" title="Henry Morton Stanley">Henry Morton Stanley</a> in 1872. Stanley circumnavigated the lake and confirmed Speke's observations in 1875.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911698_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911698-68">[66]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<dl><dd>Krapf went to East Africa in the service of the English Church Missionary Society, arriving at <a href="/wiki/Mombasa" title="Mombasa">Mombasa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a> in 1844 and staying in East Africa until 1853. While stationed there he was the first to report the existence of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Baringo" title="Lake Baringo">Lake Baringo</a> and a sighting of the snow-clad <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilimanjaro" title="Kilimanjaro">Kilimanjaro</a>. Krapf, during his travels, collected information from the Arab traders operating inland from the coast. From the traders Krapf and his companions learned of great lakes and snow-capped mountains, which Krapf claimed to have seen for himself, much to the ridicule of English explorers who could not believe the idea of snow on the equator. However, Krapf was correct and had seen Mounts Kilimanjaro and Kenya, the first European to do so.</dd></dl>
<ul><li><i>Egypt, Soudan and Central Africa: With Explorations From Khartoum on the White Nile to the Regions of the Equator, Being Sketches from Sixteen Years' Travel</i>, <a href="/wiki/John_Petherick" title="John Petherick">John Petherick</a>. <a href="/wiki/William_Blackwood" title="William Blackwood">William Blackwood</a>, Edinburgh; 1861</li></ul>
<dl><dd>Petherick was a well known Welsh traveler in East Central Africa where he had adopted the profession of mining engineer. This work describes sixteen years of his travel throughout Africa. In 1845, he entered the service of Mehemet Ali, and was employed in examining Upper Egypt, Nubia, the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> coast and <a href="/wiki/Kordofan" title="Kordofan">Kordofan</a> in an unsuccessful search for coal. In 1848, he left the Egyptian service and established himself at El Obeid as a trader and was, at the same time made British Consul for the Sudan. In 1853, he removed to <a href="/wiki/Khartoum" title="Khartoum">Khartoum</a> and became an ivory trader. He traveled extensively in the <a href="/wiki/Bahr_el_Ghazal_(region_of_South_Sudan)" title="Bahr el Ghazal (region of South Sudan)">Bahr-el-Ghazal</a> region, then almost unknown, exploring the <a href="/wiki/Jur_River" title="Jur River">Jur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yalo" title="Yalo">Yalo</a> and other affluents of the <a href="/wiki/Ghazal" title="Ghazal">Ghazal</a> and in 1858 he penetrated the <a href="/wiki/Zande_people" title="Zande people">Niam-Niam</a> country. Petherick's additions to the knowledge of natural history were considerable, being responsible for the discovery of a number of new species. In 1859, he returned to England where he became acquainted with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Speke" title="John Speke">John Speke</a>, then arranging for an expedition to discover the source of the Nile. While in England, Petherick married and published this account of his travels. He got the idea to join Speke in his travels, and in this volume is an actual subscription and list of subscribers to raise money to send Petherick to join Speke. His subsequent adventures as a consul in Africa were published in a later work.</dd></dl>
<ul><li><i>Journal of the Discovery of the Source of the Nile</i>, <a href="/wiki/John_Hanning_Speke" title="John Hanning Speke">John Hanning Speke</a>. <a href="/wiki/William_Blackwood" title="William Blackwood">William Blackwood</a>, Edinburgh, 1863; Harper &amp; Brothers, New York; 1864</li></ul>
<dl><dd>Speke had previously made an expedition with Sir Richard Burton under the auspices of the Indian government, during which Speke was convinced that he had discovered the source of the Nile. Burton, however, disagreed and ridiculed Speke's account. Speke set off on another expedition, recounted here, in the company of Captain Grant. During the course of this expedition he not only produced further evidence for his discoveries but he also met (later Sir) <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Baker" title="Samuel Baker">Samuel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Florence_Baker" title="Florence Baker">Florence Baker</a>. Speke and Burton provided them with essential information which helped Baker in the discovery of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Albert_Nyanza" title="Albert Nyanza">Albert Nyanza</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[88]</a></sup> The importance of Speke's discoveries can hardly be overestimated. In discovering the source reservoir of the Nile he succeeded in solving the problem of all ages; he and Grant were the first Europeans to cross Equatorial Eastern Africa and gained for the world a knowledge of about 800 km (500 mi) of a portion of Eastern Africa previously totally unknown.</dd></dl>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes_and_references">Notes and references</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nile_composite_NASA.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2325" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="988" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Nile_composite_NASA.jpg/170px-Nile_composite_NASA.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Nile_composite_NASA.jpg/255px-Nile_composite_NASA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Nile_composite_NASA.jpg/340px-Nile_composite_NASA.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nile_composite_NASA.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Composite_image" title="Composite image">Composite</a> satellite image of the White Nile</div></div></div>
<dl><dt>Notes</dt></dl>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-length-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-length_1-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-length_1-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-length_1-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">The length of the Nile is usually said to be about 6,650 km (4,130 mi),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica-nile_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-nile-5">[3]</a></sup> but reported values lie anywhere between 5,499 km (3,417 mi) and 7,088 km (4,404 mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-liu-etal-river-length_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-liu-etal-river-length-6">[4]</a></sup> The measurements of many rivers' lengths are only approximations and may differ from each other because there are many factors that determine the calculated length of a river, such as the position of the geographical source and the mouth, the scale of measurement, and the technique used to measure length (see also <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_rivers_by_length" title="List of rivers by length">List of rivers by length</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-liu-etal-river-length_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-liu-etal-river-length-6">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-river-source_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-river-source-10">[8]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar">النيل</span>, <small><a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Arabic" title="Romanization of Arabic">romanized</a>: </small><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">an-Nīl</i></span>, <small>Arabic pronunciation: </small><span class="IPA" lang="ar-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Arabic" title="Help:IPA/Arabic">[an'niːl]</a></span>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bohairic" title="Bohairic">Bohairic</a> <a href="/wiki/Coptic_language" title="Coptic language">Coptic</a>: <span lang="cop">ⲫⲓⲁⲣⲟ</span> <small>Pronounced </small><span class="IPA" lang="cop-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">[pʰjaˈro]</a></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[1]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Luganda_language" title="Luganda language">Luganda</a>: <i lang="lg">Kiira</i> <small>Ganda pronunciation: </small><span class="IPA" lang="lg-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">[ki:ra]</a></span>, <a href="/wiki/Nobiin_language" title="Nobiin language">Nobiin</a>: Áman Dawū<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-3">[2]</a></sup></span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-54"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-54">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Although the ancestral Sahara Desert initially developed at least 7 million years ago, it grew during interglacial periods and shrank during glacial ones. The growth of the current Sahara began about 6,000 <a href="/wiki/Before_Present" title="Before Present">years ago</a>. <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFSchuster,_Mathieu2006">Schuster, Mathieu;  et al. <li>Tvedt, Terje, ed. <i>The River Nile in the Post-Colonial Age: Conflict and Cooperation Among the Nile Basin Countries</i> (I.B. Tauris, 2010) 293 pages; studies of the river's finite resources as shared by multiple nations in the post-colonial era; includes research by scholars from Burundi, Congo, Egypt, Ethiopia, Kenya, Rwanda, Sudan, Tanzania, and Uganda.</li>
